				<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body" aria-labelledby="firstHeading" data-mw-ve-target-container>
					<div class="vector-body-before-content">
							<div class="mw-indicators">
		</div>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
					</div>
					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><p>

As of February 2024<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=List_of_chemical_elements&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, 118 <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical elements</a> have been <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_chemical_element_discoveries" class="mw-redirect" title="Timeline of chemical element discoveries">identified</a> and named officially by <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">IUPAC</a>. A chemical element, often simply called an element, is a type of <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atom</a> which has a specific  number of <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> in its <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">atomic nucleus</a> (i.e., a specific <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a>, or <i>Z</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The definitive visualisation of all 118 elements is the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table of the elements</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_periodic_table" title="History of the periodic table">history</a> along the principles of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Periodic law">periodic law</a> was one of the founding developments of modern <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>. It is a tabular arrangement of the elements by their chemical properties that usually uses abbreviated <a href="/wiki/Chemical_symbol" title="Chemical symbol">chemical symbols</a> in place of full element names, but the linear list format presented here is also useful. Like the periodic table, the list below organizes the elements by the number of protons in their atoms; it can also be organized by other properties, such as <a href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" class="mw-redirect" title="Atomic weight">atomic weight</a>, <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">electronegativity</a>. For more detailed information about the origins of element names, see <a href="/wiki/List_of_chemical_element_name_etymologies" title="List of chemical element name etymologies">List of chemical element name etymologies</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
